The Epicycle's Contingent Position
Razi's fourth aspect: the epicycle's arbitrary location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the analogy of a gem in a ring to argue that the epicycle's specific location in the eccentric orb is contingent.

Core Idea
All parts are equal, so the epicycle's specific spot is arbitrary.

The fourth aspect focuses on the epicycle (فلك التدوير) embedded in the thickness of the eccentric orb. Razi points out that the epicycle's position is like a gem set in a ring; given that all parts of the sphere are identical in nature, there is no inherent reason for its specific location, indicating contingency. Let's examine this analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the analogy of the gem and ring.
2
Understand the analogy
The epicycle is embedded in one specific spot in the eccentric orb, just like a gem is set in a particular place on a ring.
3
Apply the principle of indifference
Since all parts of the sphere are equal, the specific location cannot be necessary; it must be possible, pointing to a choice by an external agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : زعموا : أن كل كرة من كرات الأفلاك فهو بسيط وهذا يوجب عليهم الإمكان من وجوه كثيرة : 
أحدها: أنه لما كان جميع أجزاء تلك الكرة متساوية في الطبيعة والماهية ، كان حصول النقرة التي حصل فيها الكوكب في جانب معين من ذلك الفلك دون سائر الجوانب ، من الجائزات 
وثانيها : لما كانت طبائع الكرات بسائط كانت طبيعة كل واحد من المتهمين اللذين ينفصلان عن الفلك المميل بسبب انفصال الفلك الخارج المركب عنه : طبيعة بسيطة . مع أن أحد الجانبين ، اختص بالرقة ، والجانب الثاني ، اختص بالثخن. فيكون ذلك من الجائزات . 
وثالثها : إن طبيعة السطح الأعلى من كل فلك تكون مساوية لطبيعة السطح الأسفل منه ، وكل ما جاز على الشيء ، جاز على مثله . 
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كما جاز على كرة عطارد أن تماس كرة القمر بمقعرها ، وجب أن يجوز عليها أن تماسها بمحدبها ، ومتى ثبت هذا الجواز كان جواز الخرق والتفرق ، والتمزق لازما 
ورابعها : زعموا أن فلك التدوير مركوز في ثخن الفلك الخارج عن المركز ، كما يكون الفص في الخاتم ، وحينئذ يعود السؤال : إنه لم حصلت كرة التدوير في ذلك الموضع دون سائر المواضع مع أن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة في كرة الفلك متساوية في الطبيعة ؟ 
واعلم أن الاستقصاء في شرح هذه الأحوال سيأتي في باب الاستدلال على الصانع الحكيم بحدوث الصفات. فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معاقد الدلائل المأخوذة من أجرام الأفلاك بحسب إمكان صفاتها
The thirteenth: they claim that every sphere of the celestial spheres is simple, and this necessitates possibility for them from many aspects: 
One of them: that since all parts of that sphere are equal in nature and essence, the occurrence of the depression in which the planet occurs in a specific side of that orb rather than the other sides is among possible things. 
The second: that since the natures of spheres are simple, the nature of each of the two [contiguous?] that separate from the inclined orb due to the separation of the outer composite orb from it is a simple nature, while one of the two sides is assigned thinness and the other side is assigned thickness; so that is among possible things. 
The third: that the nature of the upper surface of each sphere is equal to the nature of its lower surface, and whatever is possible for a thing is possible for its like. 
If this is established, we say: just as it is possible for the sphere of Mercury to touch the sphere of the Moon by its concavity, it must be possible for it to touch it by its convexity, and when this possibility is established, the possibility of breaking, splitting, and tearing becomes necessary. 
The fourth: they claim that the epicycle is embedded in the thickness of the eccentric orb, like the gem in the ring, and then the question returns: why did the epicycle occur in that position rather than others, given that all supposed parts in the sphere's sphere are equal in nature? 
And know that the detailed explanation of these states will come in the chapter of inference to the Wise Creator from the temporality of attributes. So this is the indication to the knots of proofs taken from the celestial bodies in accordance with the possibility of their attributes.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth argument, which contends that if celestial spheres are simple (بسيط), then many contingent features arise. The author lists four specific aspects. First, since all parts of a sphere are identical in nature, the planet's specific location in a depression on one side is contingent. Second, with simple natures, the separation of two contiguous parts from the inclined orb results in one being thin and the other thick, a contingent quality. Third, since upper and lower surfaces of a sphere are equal in nature, what is possible for one is possible for the other; thus, if the sphere of Mercury can touch the Moon's sphere from the concave side, it could also from the convex side, leading to the possibility of rupture—a contingent state. Fourth, the epicycle's specific position within the eccentric orb, like a gem in a ring, is arbitrary given all parts are equal, again showing contingency. The author remarks that these arguments are brief indications; full elaboration will come later in discussing proofs from the temporality of attributes.

Key Terms
فلك التدوير
فلك الخارج عن المركز
الفص
الخاتم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The epicycle's position is necessary due to astronomical laws.
Correction: Razi argues that since all parts are equal, there is no necessity; the actual position is one of many possibilities.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Razi use in the fourth aspect?
A) A sphere in a cube
B) A gem in a ring
C) A star in the sky
D) A planet in its orbit

Key Takeaway
The epicycle's specific location, like all contingent features, points to an external chooser.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the epicycle's arbitrary position points to an external chooser. Where in your own life do you assume things are 'just the way they are' and forget that each specific detail was chosen by Allah? Will you consciously acknowledge His choice in those matters today?
?
The argument depends on the fact that all parts are equal, so any one position is possible, not necessary. When you look at your own circumstances—your family, your skills, your trials—do you see them as the only possible outcome, or as intentionally selected by Allah from countless alternatives? How does that change your gratitude or patience?
?
If you had to explain this proof to someone who says 'everything just happened naturally,' what in your own heart would prevent you from truly believing that Allah's choice is behind every specific detail? What step will you take today to strengthen that certainty?

The Argument from Elemental Spheres
Razi presents the argument from elemental spheres to the necessity of a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's inductive argument from the contingent attributes of the elemental spheres to the necessity of a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār).

Core Idea
The orderly variation of the elements demands a free chooser.

Razi presents the second major proof by examining the four elemental spheres—earth, water, air, fire—and their distinct qualities and positions. He argues that these variations are contingent (possible), and contingency requires a deliberate agent who chooses among possibilities. This sets the stage for a detailed classification of opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to 'الفاعل المختار'. Then read the translation.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms 'العناصر', 'الجائزات', 'الفاعل المختار'. Understand that 'الجائزات' means possible or contingent.
3
Trace the logical flow
Razi says: the spheres have different attributes and positions; these differences are possible; therefore they must be by the decree of a free agent. Understand that 'must' indicates a rational necessity.
4
Note the transition
The phrase 'فإن قيل' signals an anticipated objection, which will require a premise about worldviews. This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو الدليل المأخوذ من إمكان صفات العناصر . فنقول : كرة الأرض محفوفة بالماء ، والماء بالهواء ، والهواء بالنار . فاختلاف هذه الأجرام العنصرية بهذه الصفات وبهذه الأحياز من الجائزات فوجب أن تكون بتقدير الفاعل المختار . فإن قيل : السؤال على هذا الدليل يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بمقدمة ، وهي أن للناس في وجود هذا العالم المحسوس قولان : منهم من قال : إن هذه السموات ، وهذه الكواكب ، وهذه العناصر قديمة بحسب ذواتها ، وبحسب أشكالها ، وترتيب أمكنتها . ومنهم من قال : إنها محدثة. أما القائلون بالقول الأول فهم فريقان : منهم من يقول : إنها واجبة الوجود لذواتها ، وهي غنية عن السبب والمؤثر، ومنهم من يقول : إنها ممكنة الوجود بذواتها، واجبة الوجود بسبب تأثير علة قديمة في وجودها ، وزعم هؤلاء : أن ذلك المؤثر موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار ، ويسمون ذلك المؤثر بأنه أثر بعلة . وأما القائلون بأن هذا العالم المحسوس محدث ، فلهم قولان : منهم من يقول : إن هذا العالم المحسوس محدث بحسب الذات والصفات فهذه الذوات المتميزة محدثة ، وتركيب السموات والعناصر منها أيضا محدث. وهؤلاء هم أكثر أهل الملل والنحل . ومنهم من
As for the second section: it is the proof taken from the possibility of the attributes of the elements. We say: the sphere of the earth is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire. So the variation of these elemental bodies in these attributes and in these positions is among possible things, so it must be by the decree of a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār). If it is said: the objection to this proof must be preceded by a premise, which is that people have two statements regarding the existence of this sensible world: some said that these heavens, these stars, and these elements are eternal with respect to their essences, their forms, and the arrangement of their places; and some said that they are created. As for those who hold the first statement, they are two groups: some say they are necessary of existence in themselves, and they are independent of cause and effect; and some say they are possible of existence in themselves, necessary of existence due to the influence of an eternal cause in their existence, and these claim that that influencer is necessitating by essence (mūjib bi-l-dhāt) not an agent by choice, and they call that influencer 'effect by a cause'. And as for those who say this sensible world is created, they have two statements: some say that this sensible world is created in terms of essence and attributes, so these distinct essences are created, and the composition of the heavens and elements from them is also created. These are the majority of the people of religions and sects. And among them are those who...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage begins the second main argument: the proof from the possibility of elemental attributes. The author describes the concentric arrangement of the elemental spheres: earth, water, air, fire. Their specific qualities and positions are contingent (min al-jāʾizāt), necessitating a free chooser (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār). Anticipating an objection, the author notes that the objection itself requires a premise: classification of views on the world's existence. He then outlines two main views: (1) those who say the world is eternal, and (2) those who say it is created. The eternalists are subdivided into those who say the world is necessary in itself (independently existing) and those who say it is possible in itself but necessary due to an eternal cause (the philosophers, who posit a necessitating cause not acting by choice). The creationists are subdivided: one group holds that the world is created in both essence and attributes, including the distinct essences and their composition; this is the view of most religious people. The second group (cut off) would presumably be another variation. This classification sets up the objection and subsequent defense of his argument.

Key Terms
العناصر
الفاعل المختار
الجائزات
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument from elements is purely empirical and not rational.
Correction: Razi's argument is rational: he uses observation to establish contingency, then applies the rational principle that every contingent requires a determinant.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the variation of the elemental spheres?
A) The spheres are eternal
B) The spheres are possible in themselves
C) The variation must be by the decree of a free agent
D) The spheres are caused by a necessitating cause

Key Takeaway
The contingent variation of the elements proves the existence of a free Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the very arrangement of the earth, water, air, and fire around you is not necessary but chosen by God, then every aspect of your existence is a deliberate act of His will. How does this realization affect your perception of the current moment you are in? Will you live this moment as if it were a random accident or a gift from a free and loving Creator?
?
Razi argues that the variation in the elements must be from a free agent. Consider one variable in your own life that you often take for granted—like your health, your location, or your abilities. If that variable could have been otherwise, does it not demand a personal response of gratitude and submission? What one specific action will you take today to acknowledge that God freely chose your circumstances?
?
The proof from the elements shows that nothing in the physical world is self-necessitating; everything is contingent. If you truly believed that every atom in your body is sustained by God's free choice, how would it change your attachment to material things? Can you identify one possession or worry that you treat as if it were necessary, and surrender it in your heart?

Classification of Views on the World's Existence
Razi classifies opinions on the world's existence into eternalist and creationist camps, with subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major positions on whether the world is eternal or created, and their subdivisions as outlined by Razi.

Core Idea
The world's existence is debated: eternal vs. created, with further nuances on necessity and causation.

Before addressing objections to his argument, Razi provides a detailed classification of philosophical and theological views on the world's existence. This taxonomy is crucial for understanding the different challenges his proof faces. He divides opinions into two main camps: those who hold the world eternal and those who hold it created, each with sub-camps.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read from 'فإن قيل' to the end of the passage. Note the two main groups and their subdivisions.
The text ends abruptly, so we have incomplete information.
2
Identify the eternalist views
Eternalists are divided: (1) those who say the world is necessary in itself, (2) those who say it is possible in itself but necessitated by an eternal cause.
The second group are the philosophers who posit a 'mūjib bi-l-dhāt'.
3
Identify the creationist views
Creationists are divided: (1) those who say the world is created in essence and attributes (majority of religious people), and (2) a second group cut off.
4
Understand the relevance
This classification sets up the objection that the argument from elements may be refuted by those who hold different views on the world's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو الدليل المأخوذ من إمكان صفات العناصر . فنقول : كرة الأرض محفوفة بالماء ، والماء بالهواء ، والهواء بالنار . فاختلاف هذه الأجرام العنصرية بهذه الصفات وبهذه الأحياز من الجائزات فوجب أن تكون بتقدير الفاعل المختار . فإن قيل : السؤال على هذا الدليل يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بمقدمة ، وهي أن للناس في وجود هذا العالم المحسوس قولان : منهم من قال : إن هذه السموات ، وهذه الكواكب ، وهذه العناصر قديمة بحسب ذواتها ، وبحسب أشكالها ، وترتيب أمكنتها . ومنهم من قال : إنها محدثة. أما القائلون بالقول الأول فهم فريقان : منهم من يقول : إنها واجبة الوجود لذواتها ، وهي غنية عن السبب والمؤثر، ومنهم من يقول : إنها ممكنة الوجود بذواتها، واجبة الوجود بسبب تأثير علة قديمة في وجودها ، وزعم هؤلاء : أن ذلك المؤثر موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار ، ويسمون ذلك المؤثر بأنه أثر بعلة . وأما القائلون بأن هذا العالم المحسوس محدث ، فلهم قولان : منهم من يقول : إن هذا العالم المحسوس محدث بحسب الذات والصفات فهذه الذوات المتميزة محدثة ، وتركيب السموات والعناصر منها أيضا محدث. وهؤلاء هم أكثر أهل الملل والنحل . ومنهم من

Commentary
This passage begins the second main argument: the proof from the possibility of elemental attributes. The author describes the concentric arrangement of the elemental spheres: earth, water, air, fire. Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the view of the majority of the people of religions and sects about the world?
A) The world is eternal and necessary in itself
B) The world is created in both essence and attributes
C) The world is eternal but caused by a necessitating cause
D) The world is eternal in essence but created in attributes

Key Takeaway
Razi classifies worldviews to prepare for a refutation of objections to his argument from elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi classifies those who see the world as necessary in themselves. Have you ever lived as if the universe were self-sufficient, forgetting that it is contingent upon Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that every star, every atom, is sustained by Him every moment?
?
The passage distinguishes between a necessitating cause (mūjib bi-l-dhāt) and a free agent. Which concept of God do you tend to default to in your prayers: a God who is bound by a fixed order, or a God who freely responds? How might this affect your reliance on Him?
?
Razi notes that the majority of religious people hold the world is created in essence and attributes. Yet he also notes a different view among creationists. Does it unsettle you that there are different views among Muslims on such a fundamental issue? How do you handle theological diversity without falling into skepticism?

Democritus' Atomism and Aristotle's Refutation
Exposition of Democritus' atomist theory and Aristotle's fourfold refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist cosmology of Democritus and the key arguments Aristotle used to refute it based on self-motion, void, and indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Atomism offers a purely mechanical universe, but Aristotle dismantles its three foundational premises.

This passage presents a detailed materialist worldview that explains the origin of the cosmos without a Creator. It then provides Aristotle's refutation, showing that the theory relies on premises already proven false in natural science. Understanding this debate sharpens one's grasp of the necessity of a divine cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the exposition of Democritus' theory
Read the first part of the passage describing how eternal atoms move in void and generate the celestial spheres and elements through random collisions.
Notice that the theory accounts for all cosmic phenomena without invoking a Creator.
2
Identify the challenge to theists
The atomists assert that unless their explanation is disproven, the argument from design for a wise Creator fails. Pause to appreciate the force of this challenge.
This sets up the need for a refutation.
3
Examine Aristotle's three refutations
The passage lists three explicit refutations: (1) self-motion of bodies is impossible, (2) the void does not exist, (3) indivisible parts are false. Each is said to have been proven elsewhere.
These refutations target the very foundations of atomism.
4
Note the incomplete fourth refutation
The text begins a fourth aspect but cuts off. Consider why the three refutations are already sufficient to deem the doctrine corrupt.
The author concludes that since the three principles are false, the whole doctrine is corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول : إن ذوات الأجسام قديمة ، أما التركيبات فهي محدثة كانت جميع والقائلون بهذا القول أيضا . فريقان : منهم من يقول : إن تلك الأجزاء كانت في الأزل متحركة بالطبع في الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له ، ومنهم من يقول : إنها كانت ساكنة واقفة ، أما القائلون بكونها متحركة فهم أصحاب ديمقراطيس ، زعموا : أن عنصر العالم الجسماني له أجزاء لا تتجزأ بحسب الوقوع ومتجزئة بحسب الوهم ، وزعموا : أنها على صور الكرات ، والكرة إذا وقعت على الخلاء المتساوي المتشابه فإنه يمتنع بقاؤها ساكنة ، لأنه لما الأحياز بالنسبة إليه على التساوي كان إبقاؤه على الوضع الواحد رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أن تلك الأجزاء يجب كونها متحركة لذواتها حركات مضطربة غير مناسبة ، ثم اتفق لتلك الأجزاء المضطربة أن تصادمت على وجه خاص فيما بعد ، وتدافعت في حركاتها، فالتوى العبض على البعض ، واعتمد البعض على البعض فتولد من تصادمها وتدافعها ، والتواء بعضها على البعض هذه الأجرام الفلكية . وهذا هو السبب في تولد الأجرام الفلكية. فما لم تقيموا الدلالة على بطلان هذا الاحتمال ، لا يحصل مقصودكم من إسناد تخليق هذا العالم إلى القادر الحكيم ولا يقال : ولم حصل كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء الكروية في حيزه المعين ؟ لأنا كنا قد ذكرنا : أن كل واحد منها كان متحركا من الأزل إلى الأبد فحصول كل حالة لاحقة، إنما كان لأجل أن الحالة السابقة ، أعدت المادة لحصول تلك الحالة اللاحقة . فهذا تقرير قولهم في كيفية تولد الأفلاك . وأما كيفية تولد العناصر ، فقالوا : إنه لما تولد جرم الفلك بالطريق المذكور واستدار. كان باطنه مملوءاً من الأجسام ، والحركة موجبة للسخونة ، والفلك لأجل حركته القوية يوجب غاية السخونة واللطافة ، فيما بلاصقه من الأجسام ، ويوجب غاية البرودة والكثافة في الأجسام التي تكون في غاية البعد منه ، وهي الأجسام الحاصلة عند القرب من المركز فلهذا السبب صار الجسم العنصري المماس لجرم الفلك في غاية اللطافة والحرارة ، وهو النار ، وصار الجسم العنصري الذي يكون في غاية البعد من الفلك في غاية الكثافة والبرودة ، وهو الأرض ، والجسم المتصل بالنار ، وهو الهواء لكونه شبيها بالنار مع أنه أقل لطافة من النار ، والجسم المتصل بالأرض ، هو الماء لكونه شبيها بالأرض في الكثافة والبرودة ، مع أنه أقل كثافة وبردا من الأرض ، فهذا هو السبب في ترتيب هذه العناصر ، ثم لما استدارت الأفلاك بحركاتها المختلفة على هذه العناصر المختلفة ، اقتضت حركات الأفلاك تمزيج بعض أجزاء هذه العناصر ببعض ، فتولدت المواليد الثلاثة من المعادن والنباتات والحيوان فهذا تفصيل مذهب دميقراطيس في هذا الباب قالوا : وما لم تبطلوا بالدليل فساد هذا الاحتمال لم يحصل مقصودكم البتة من الاستدلال بأحوال الأفلاك والعناصر ، على وجود الصانع الحكيم . والجواب : أن أرسطاطاليس قد أبطل هذا القول من وجوه : أحدها : إنه بين أن كون الجسم متحركا لذاته محال ، وقد سبقت هذه على سبيل الاستقصاء في العلم الطبيعي وثانيها : إن هذا بناء على إثبات الخلاء خارج العالم وقد سبقت هذه أيضا على سبيل الاستقصاء ، ثالثهما : إن هذا بناء على أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وقد سبقت هذه المسألة أيضا على سبيل الاستقصاء . ولما كانت هذه القواعد الثلاثة باطلة عند أرسطاطاليس ، لا جرم حكم بفساد هذا المذهب . ورابعها : إن
He says: The essences of bodies are eternal, but compositions are originated. All [bodies] were... And those who hold this saying are two groups: some say that those parts were in pre-eternity moving by nature in infinite void, and some say they were stationary and still. As for those who say they were moving, they are the followers of Democritus. They claimed that the element of the corporeal world has parts that are indivisible in terms of occurrence and divisible in terms of estimation, and they claimed that they are spherical in shape. And if a sphere falls on the void that is equal (similar), it is impossible for it to remain stationary, because since the positions (places) relative to it are equal, keeping it in one specific position would be a preference for one of the two possible sides over the other without a preponderator. And once this is established, it becomes evident that those parts must be moving by themselves with erratic and non-uniform motions. Then it happened for those erratic parts to collide in a specific manner later, and they repelled each other in their motions, and some twisted over others, and some relied on others, and from their collision, repulsion, and twisting of some over others, these celestial bodies were generated. And this is the cause of the generation of celestial bodies. So, unless you establish proof of the falsity of this possibility, your purpose of attributing the creation of this world to the Wise Powerful (Creator) will not be achieved. And it cannot be said: Why did each of those spherical parts come to be in its specific place? Because we have already mentioned that each one of them was moving from eternity to eternity, so each subsequent state came about only because the previous state prepared the matter for the arrival of that subsequent state. This is the presentation of their view on how the celestial spheres were generated. As for how the elements were generated, they said: When the celestial sphere was generated by the mentioned method and became spherical, its interior was filled with bodies, and motion causes heat. The sphere, due to its strong motion, causes extreme heat and subtlety in the bodies adjacent to it, and causes extreme cold and density in the bodies that are at the farthest distance from it, which are the bodies that occur near the center. For this reason, the elemental body that is in contact with the celestial sphere became extremely subtle and hot, which is fire; and the elemental body that is at the farthest distance from the sphere became extremely dense and cold, which is earth; and the body connected to fire, which is air, because it resembles fire while being less subtle than fire; and the body connected to earth, which is water, because it resembles earth in density and coldness while being less dense and cold than earth. This is the reason for the arrangement of these elements. Then, when the spheres rotated with their different motions over these different elements, the motions of the spheres necessitated the mixing of some parts of these elements with others, and thus the three productions (minerals, plants, and animals) were generated. This is the detailed account of the doctrine of Democritus in this matter. They said: And unless you prove with evidence the corruption of this possibility, your purpose of inferring from the states of the celestial spheres and elements the existence of the Wise Maker will never be achieved. The answer: Aristotle refuted this saying in several aspects: First: He demonstrated that a body being moving by itself is impossible, and this has already been exhaustively covered in natural science. Second: This is based on affirming the void outside the world, and this has also been exhaustively covered. Third: This is based on the idea that these sensible bodies are composed of indivisible parts, and this issue has also been exhaustively covered. Since these three principles are false according to Aristotle, he necessarily judged that this doctrine is corrupt. And fourth: ...
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a materialist cosmology attributed to Democritus and then provides Aristotle's refutation of it. The passage begins by stating the materialist claim: the essences (selves) of bodies are eternal, but their compositions are originated. Two groups are mentioned: one holds that atoms were always in motion in infinite void, the other that they were initially at rest. The passage focuses on the first group, the followers of Democritus. They assert that the material world consists of indivisible atoms (parts that cannot be divided in reality but can be conceptually divided) that are spherical. They argue that a sphere in a uniform void cannot remain stationary because there is no reason favoring one position over another; thus, atoms must be in perpetual, erratic motion. By chance collisions and entanglements, these atoms form the celestial spheres and the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) in concentric layers due to the heating effect of the rotating spheres. From the mixing of elements arise minerals, plants, and animals. The materialists then challenge the theist: unless you disprove this alternative explanation, your argument from the order of the heavens and elements to a wise Creator fails. The reply (attributed to Aristotle) refutes the theory on three (and a fourth) grounds. First, a body moving by itself is impossible—a point already proven in natural science. Second, the theory assumes a void outside the world, which has also been disproven. Third, it assumes atoms (indivisible parts), which is also false. Because these three foundations are invalid, Aristotle judges the entire doctrine corrupt. The fourth refutation is begun but the passage ends abruptly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Democritus' random motion of atoms is a plausible alternative to creation by a wise Creator.
Correction: The passage shows that Aristotle has already refuted the foundational premises (self-motion, void, indivisible parts) in natural science, so the theory is not a tenable alternative.

Checkpoint
How many 'aspects' (wujuh) does Aristotle use to refute Democritus' theory according to the text?

C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Aristotle refutes atomism by showing its reliance on three false premises: self-motion of bodies, existence of void, and indivisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that even the most sophisticated materialist explanation (like Democritus' atomism) can be refuted by sound reasoning. In your own life, have you ever accepted an explanation for something (an event, a relationship, a habit) that you later realized was based on false premises? What was that explanation, and what false premise did it rely on? Commit to re-examining one such belief today.
?
Aristotle refuted the atomists by pointing out that their premises were already proven false. Are there any foundational beliefs in your faith that you hold without having examined the evidence? Identify one belief about Allah's existence or attributes that you have never critically thought through, and set aside time this week to study its proof.
?
The materialists in this passage challenged: 'Unless you disprove this possibility, your argument for a Creator fails.' Have you ever felt pressured to 'disprove' every alternative to your faith before you can be confident? How does this passage empower you to recognize that some alternatives rest on false foundations, and that your faith does not require you to refute every possibility—only to recognize which premises are sound?

The Standard Kalam Cosmological Argument
The author outlines the standard kalam cosmological argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the standard kalam cosmological argument, including how the temporality of bodies leads to the necessity of a self-sufficient Creator.

Core Idea
Temporality implies contingency, contingency demands a cause, and the cause must be eternal.

In this passage, the author outlines the backbone of the classical kalam proof for God's existence. The argument moves from the observable temporality of bodies to the necessity of an eternal, self-sufficient Creator. The author emphasizes that this is the method relied upon by the majority of mutakallimun, and he distinguishes two key sub-arguments: proving that bodies are temporal, and proving that every temporal requires an agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall argument structure
Read the passage and note the main claim: the majority of mutakallimun rely on this single method. Then trace the logical chain from bodies to a necessary being.
The argument is presented in two parts: proving temporality and proving the need for a cause.
2
Understand the first premise: bodies are temporal
The author states that they establish evidence for the temporality of bodies, but the detailed proof is deferred. Accept this as a given for now.
The focus here is on the logical connection from temporality to a creator.
3
Analyze the second premise: every temporal needs a cause
The author refines this: every temporal is contingent, and every contingent needs an efficient cause. This avoids the simplistic 'everything has a cause' and grounds it in contingency.
Contingency means something could not exist; its existence requires an external cause.
4
Follow the conclusion: the cause must be eternal
If the cause itself were temporal, we would have infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, the ultimate cause must be eternal and self-sufficient, which is the desired conclusion.
This is the step that establishes the existence of God as the necessary being.

Source Text

الفصل الثامن عشر
في
إثبات العالم بوجود الإكيد تعالى بناء على التمسك بحدوث القوات

أعلم : أن جمهور المتكلمين ، لا يعولون ، إلا على هذا الطريق . وذلك لأنهم يقيمون الدلالة على كون الأجسام محدثة . وحينئذ يقولون : كل جسم محدث ، وكل محدث فله علة وصانع . ينتج : أن كل جسم فله فاعل وصانع .
ثم هذا الدليل إنما يتم إذا قلنا : وذلك الفاعل ، إن كان محدثاً لزم التسلسل أو الدور ، وإن كان قديماً ، فهو واجب الوجود لذاته . وهو المطلوب.
فأما الكلام في إثبات حدوث الأجسام ، فسيأتي على سبيل الاستقصاء . وأما الكلام في قولنا : كل محدث فلا بد له من فاعل وصانع . فموضعه ههنا .
وهو : أن يقال : كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل ما كان ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فله فاعل وصانع ، ينتج أن كل محدث فله فاعل . وفي هذا الطريق يستدل بحدوث الأجسام على كونها ممكنة الوجود ، ثم يستدل بامكانها هذا ، على افتقارهما الى الفاعل .
ونحن نذكر في هذا الفصل : كيفية تقرير هذا الوجه فنقول : كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود، وكل ممكن الوجود فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر وإنما قلنا : إن
Chapter Eighteen: On Proving the World by the Existence of God Almighty based on adhering to the temporality of powers. Know that the majority of the mutakallimun rely only on this method. This is because they establish evidence that bodies are temporal. Then they say: Every body is temporal, and every temporal has a cause and a maker. It follows: Every body has an agent and a maker. Then this proof is only complete if we say: That agent, if it were temporal, then infinite regress or circularity would follow, and if it were eternal, then it is necessary of existence in itself. And that is what is sought. As for the discourse on proving the temporality of bodies, it will come later in detail. And as for the discourse on our statement: Every temporal necessarily has an agent and a maker, its place is here. It is: That it is said: Every temporal is contingent in itself, and everything that is contingent in itself has an agent and a maker, so it follows that every temporal has an agent. And in this way, one infers from the temporality of bodies to their contingency, then from this contingency to their need for an agent. And we will mention in this chapter the manner of establishing this aspect, so we say: Every temporal is contingent in existence, and every contingent in existence is in need of an efficient cause. And we only say that...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage introduces the standard kalām cosmological argument. The author states that the majority of mutakallimūn rely on a single method: proving that bodies are temporal, then arguing that every temporal needs a cause, and that cause must be eternal (otherwise infinite regress), therefore the First Cause is eternal and self-sufficient. The author then distinguishes two sub-arguments: (1) proving the temporality of bodies, to be handled later, and (2) proving that every temporal needs an agent, which is discussed here. He refines the second premise: every temporal is contingent (mumkin al-wujūd) in itself, and every contingent needs an efficient cause. This establishes that every temporal has an agent. The author promises to elaborate further on this formulation.

Misconception: The proof is simply 'everything has a cause'.
Correction: The proof specifically argues that temporal things are contingent, and contingency demands a cause that is eternal; this avoids infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The standard kalam argument shows that the temporality of the universe leads necessarily to the existence of an eternal, self-sufficient Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you genuinely feel your contingency (imkān) every moment? The argument shows that you are not necessary—your existence is borrowed. How would your daily attitude change if you truly internalized that you are constantly in need of the Eternal Cause?
?
The proof ends with a Being that is necessary in itself—dependent on nothing, yet everything depends on Him. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God needs your worship or your obedience? How can you shift your heart to know that He is al-Wājib, completely free and independent?
?
If the cause of all temporal things must be eternal and uncaused, then nothing in the universe can compel God. Does your prayer reflect submission to a God who is absolutely sovereign, or do you sometimes pray as if you are negotiating with a being who can be influenced? What would it mean to bow before a Necessary Being who owes nothing to anyone?

The Kalam Cosmological Argument: From Contingency to the Necessary
al-Razi states the kalām theologians' method for proving God's existence via contingency of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general method used by the majority of mutakallimīn to prove the existence of God through the contingency of bodies, including the key steps and the reasoning against infinite regress.

Core Idea
The kalam cosmological argument grounds God's existence in the contingency of every body.

al-Razi introduces the signature approach of the majority of Muslim theologians (mutakallimīn) for proving God's existence. This method centers on showing that every body is temporal, and every temporal thing requires a cause. The argument then eliminates the possibility of an infinite causal chain, concluding to a necessary, eternal agent. This passage is a concise blueprint of the entire proof, with detailed discussions deferred.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's concise style come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Razi uses a compressed summary style here.
2
Identify the two-part structure
The passage has two main parts: (1) establishing that bodies are temporal, and (2) applying the causal principle to conclude that every body has an agent, then analyzing the nature of that agent.
3
Understand the regress argument
Pay attention to the conditional: if the agent were temporal, it would lead to infinite regression or circularity, which are impossible. Hence the agent must be eternal and necessary.
This is the key step that forces the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the role of this passage
Ask yourself why the mutakallimīn rely on this particular way. Consider that it avoids relying on empirical observations and instead uses rational necessity.
This connects to the broader kalām approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن جمهور المتكلمين ، لا يعولون ، إلا على هذا الطريق . وذلك لأنهم يقيمون الدلالة على كون الأجسام محدثة . وحينئذ يقولون : كل جسم محدث ، وكل محدث فله علة وصانع . ينتج : أن كل جسم فله فاعل وصانع . ثم هذا الدليل إنما يتم إذا قلنا : وذلك الفاعل ، إن كان محدثاً لزم التسلسل أو الدور ، وإن كان قديماً ، فهو واجب الوجود لذاته . وهو المطلوب. فأما الكلام في إثبات حدوث الأجسام ، فسيأتي على سبيل الاستقصاء . وأما الكلام في قولنا : كل محدث فلا بد له من فاعل وصانع . فموضعه ههنا .
Know that the majority of the mutakallimīn rely only on this way. That is because they establish the proof that bodies are temporal. Then they say: every body is temporal, and every temporal has a cause and a maker. The conclusion: every body has an agent and a maker. Then this proof is only complete if we say: that agent, if it is temporal, then infinite regression or circularity would follow, and if it is eternal, then it is necessary of existence by itself. That is what is sought. As for the speech on proving the temporality of bodies, it will come exhaustively. And as for the speech on our saying: every temporal must have an agent and a maker, its place is here.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the general method used by the majority of mutakallimīn to prove the existence of God. It is a brief summary of the cosmological argument based on the contingency of bodies. The author states that they first prove that bodies are temporal, then they argue: every body is temporal, and every temporal needs a cause and a maker. This yields that every body has an agent. Then they consider the nature of this agent: if it is itself temporal, that leads to infinite regression or circularity, which are impossible. Therefore, it must be eternal and necessary of existence by itself. That is the desired conclusion. The author notes that the detailed proof of the temporality of bodies will come later, while the proof that every temporal requires a maker is the subject of the present discussion.

?

?
The argument shows that infinite regress is impossible. Where in your life have you been 'kicking the can down the road'—delaying the question of your ultimate purpose, or attributing independence to created causes? What would it mean for you to end that regress today and acknowledge the Necessary Being as your only true support?
?

Every Temporal Event Is Possible in Itself
al-Razi proves that every temporal event is possible in itself and thus needs an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that every temporal event (ḥādith) is possible in itself and therefore requires an external cause, including the precise definition of 'possible' as the essence's capacity to receive existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
A temporal event's own essence must be receptive to both existence and non-existence.

Al-Razi presents a crucial step in the kalam cosmological argument: proving that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself. He defines 'possible' not as mere conceivability but as the essence's capacity to receive both existence and non-existence. This lesson unpacks that definition and the syllogism, showing how the temporality of something implies its possibility and thus its need for a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once. Notice the logical flow: first a syllogism, then an explanation of why temporal events are possible, then a reference to a previous proof.
The argument builds on the first proof mentioned at the end.
2
Identify the syllogism
Find the major premise ('every temporal event is possible'), the minor premise ('every possible needs an agent'), and the conclusion ('every temporal event has an agent'). Understand that the passage focuses on establishing the major premise.
3
Understand the definition of 'possible'
Focus on the reasoning: a temporal event exists now but was non-existent; its essence must be receptive to both states. That receptivity is the meaning of 'possible'. Ask yourself why this definition is necessary for the proof.
This definition avoids confusion with mere conceivability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو : أن يقال : كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل ما كان ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فله فاعل وصانع ، ينتج أن كل محدث فله فاعل . وفي هذا الطريق يستدل بحدوث الأجسام على كونها ممكنة الوجود ، ثم يستدل بامكانها هذا ، على افتقارها إلى الفاعل . ونحن نذكر في هذا الفصل : كيفية تقرير هذا الوجه فنقول : كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود، وكل ممكن الوجود فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر وإنما قلنا : إن كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته وذلك لأن كل ما كان محدثاً فهو في الحال موجود ، وقد كان قبل وجوده معدوماً . ونقول : لو لم تكن حقيقته قابلة للوجود لما وجد في الحال ، ولو لم تكن الحقيقة قابلة للعدم ، لما كانت معدومة في الماضي. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما كان محدثاً ، فإن ماهيته قابلة للعدم ، وقابلة للوجود ، ولا معنى للممكن إلا ذلك فيثبت : أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته وأما بيان أن كل ما كان ممكناً لذاته فلا بد له من المؤثر : فلما مر تقريره في البرهان الأول.
And it is that one says: every temporal event is possible of existence by itself, and everything that is possible of existence by itself has an agent and a maker, resulting that every temporal event has an agent. And in this way, one infers from the temporality of bodies to their being possible of existence, then infers from this possibility to their need for an agent. And we mention in this chapter the manner of establishing this aspect, and we say: every temporal event is possible of existence, and every possible of existence is in need of the effective cause. And we said: every temporal event is possible of existence by itself, because every temporal event is at present existent, and it was non-existent before its existence. And we say: if its essence were not receptive to existence, it would not exist now, and if the essence were not receptive to non-existence, it would not have been non-existent in the past. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that every temporal event has a quiddity receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence, and there is no meaning of 'possible' except that. So it is established that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself. As for the demonstration that everything that is possible by itself must have an effective cause, its establishment has already passed in the first proof.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed exposition of the argument's second premise: that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself. The author first states the full syllogism: every temporal event is possible, and every possible needs an agent; thus every temporal event has an agent. He then explains the meaning of 'possible' by examining the state of a temporal event: it exists now but was non-existent before. Its essence must be receptive to both existence and non-existence, because if it were not receptive to existence, it could not have come to be, and if it were not receptive to non-existence, it would not have been non-existent in the past. This capacity to receive both is precisely what it means to be 'possible'. Therefore, every temporal event is possible by itself. Finally, he references a previous proof that every possible needs an effective cause, which has already been established.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'possible' means merely conceivable or not impossible.
Correction: Al-Razi defines 'possible' as the essence's real capacity to receive both existence and non-existence, based on the actual states of the object.

Checkpoint

A) Whatever is not impossible.
B) Whatever has a quiddity receptive to both existence and non-existence.
C) Whatever is conceivable.
D) Whatever comes into existence.

Key Takeaway
Every temporal event is possible because its essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence, and this possibility entails need for an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that a thing's essence must be receptive to existence for it to exist now. You exist now only because your essence was receptive to existence. But that receptivity alone is not enough—you needed an external cause. Who is that cause, and are you living as though you are self-sufficient or as though you owe your very breath to Him?
?
If you truly believed that every temporal event—including every moment of your life—is possible in itself and in constant need of a cause, would you plan your day differently? Would you seek help from the Cause of causes more often? Commit to one specific action today that reflects this dependence.

Objection and Defense: The Flip of Modal Status
Al-Razi refutes the objection with two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's response to the objection that a temporal event might flip from necessary non-existence to necessary existence, and how his two examples defend the possibility of temporal events.

Core Idea
A flip in modal status does not negate initial possibility.

Al-Razi presents an objector who suggests that temporal events might not be inherently possible but could have been necessary in one state and then flipped. To defend his position, al-Razi offers two examples—accidents and the temporal 'now'—to show that such flips are conceivable and do not undermine his original claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening objection carefully. The objector denies that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself, proposing instead that it might have been necessary of non-existence then flipped to necessary of existence.
This challenges the very definition of a temporal event being possible.
2
Understand the first example: accidents
Al-Razi gives the example of an accident that cannot persist. Its existence is possible at the first moment, but its non-existence in the second moment becomes necessary. If such a necessary non-existence after existence is conceivable, then why not a necessary existence after non-existence?
The example shows that modal status can change without contradiction.
3
Understand the second example: the 'now'
The temporal atom marking the boundary of past and future comes into existence necessarily and then ceases necessarily. This again illustrates a flip from necessary occurrence to necessary non-existence. Al-Razi argues that if this is possible for the 'now', it is possible for all temporal events.
The 'now' is a special case that mirrors the logic of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته . قوله : إنه كان معدوماً ثم وجد ، وهذا يدل على أن حقيقته قابلة للعدم والوجود ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه كان واجب العدم لذاته في الزمان الأول ، ثم انقلب واجب الوجود لذاته في الزمان الثاني؟ والذي يدل على أن الذي ذكرناه محتمل : وجوه : الأول : إن العرض عند كثير من الناس مستحيل البقاء ، وإذا كان كذلك فوجوده في الوقت الأول جائز ، ثم إن عدمه في الوقت الثاني واجب ، فإذا عقل أن يكون عدمه بعد وجوده عدماً واجباً لذاته ، بحيث يستحيل أن لا يصير معدوماً . فلم لا يجوز أن يصير وجوده بعد عدمه واجباً لذاته ، بحيث يستحيل عقلاً ، أن لا يصير موجوداً؟ الثاني : وهو أن هذا الآن الذي هو آخر الماضي ، وأول المستقبل كما وجد ، يمتنع بقاؤه ، لأن الحاضر يمتنع أن يكون غير المستقبل فإذا هذا الآن الحاضر كان معدوماً قبل حضوره ، ثم صار واجب الحدوث بعد أن كان معدوماً ، وبعد حدوثه يمتنع بقاؤه ، فصار واجب العدم، بعد أن كان موجوداً فإذا عقل هذا في الأنات ، التي هي آخر الزمان ، فلم لا يعقل مثله في جميع الحوادث ؟
If it is said: We do not grant that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself. His saying: 'It was non-existent then existed, and this indicates that its essence is receptive to non-existence and existence', we say: Why is it not permissible to say that it was necessary of non-existence by itself in the first time, then it flipped to being necessary of existence by itself in the second time? And what indicates that what we have mentioned is possible is aspects: First: That the accident according to many people is impossible of remaining. If that is the case, then its existence in the first time is possible, then its non-existence in the second time is necessary. So if it is understood that its non-existence after its existence is a non-existence that is necessary by itself, such that it is impossible that it not become non-existent, then why is it not permissible that its existence after its non-existence becomes necessary by itself, such that it is rationally impossible that it not become existent? Second: And it is that this 'now' which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, as it has come into existence, its remaining is impossible, because the present is impossible to be other than the future. So this present 'now' was non-existent before its presence, then it became necessary of occurrence after being non-existent, and after its occurrence its remaining is impossible, so it became necessary of non-existence after being existent. So if this is understood regarding the 'now's that are the end of time, why is it not understood similarly regarding all temporal events?
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's response. The objector denies that every temporal event is possible of existence by itself, suggesting an alternative: it might have been necessary of non-existence in the first moment and then flipped to necessary of existence in the second moment. This would mean the thing is not inherently possible but undergoes a change in its modal status. The author responds by offering two examples showing that such a flip is conceivable. First, consider accidents which, according to many, cannot persist. An accident exists momentarily, then its non-existence becomes necessary because it cannot remain. So it is conceivable that something's non-existence after existence is necessary. Analogously, it is conceivable that its existence after non-existence becomes necessary. Second, consider the temporal atom ('now') that marks the boundary between past and future. This 'now' comes into existence necessarily and then necessarily ceases. Both cases illustrate that something can be necessary in one state and then necessary in another. The author uses these examples to show that his original claim that the thing is possible is still plausible, because the objector's scenario does not entail impossibility. Essentially, the author is arguing that even if such flips occur, that does not contradict the fact that the temporal event, before its existence, was possible (since it was not necessary).

Correction: Al-Razi is not conceding; he is showing that the objector's scenario does not entail impossibility and his own position remains plausible.

B) That it was necessary of non-existence then became necessary of existence.
C) That it is eternal.
D) That it has no cause.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses analogies to show that even if a temporal event flips modal status, it does not refute its initial possibility.

The Necessity of a Preponderator in the Proof from Possibility
Concludes the proof from possibility, asserting the need for a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equal possibilities necessitate a preponderator and how this proof grounds the existence of a creator.

Core Idea
Equal possibilities cannot tip the scales without a preponderator.

This passage wraps up a classic kalam argument for the existence of God: the proof from possibility (imkān). The author, al-Sanusi, puts forward a necessary knowledge that something could either stay non-existent or become existent; since both outcomes are equally possible, a cause must tip the balance. Understanding this logical step is essential before engaging with any objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument sink in before you dissect it.
Notice the key terms: علم ضروري, تساوى الأمران, ترجيح بلا مرجح.
2
Identify the three claims
Break down the passage into its three core claims: (1) necessary knowledge of two possibilities, (2) their equality, (3) impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.
These three form the backbone of the proof.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author says this is the intended meaning of the proof and that objections fall away. Ask yourself: why does equal possibility force the need for an external cause?
This connects to the broader cosmological argument.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Take the checkpoint quiz to see if you have grasped the core logical step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنه كان يجوز بقاؤه على ذلك العدم الأصلي وكان يجوز تبدل ذلك العدم بالوجود ، ولما تساوى الأمران، امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . فهذا هو المراد من هذا الدليل . وعند الوقوف عليه يظهر أن السؤالات المذكورة ساقطة .
For necessary knowledge is obtained that it was permissible for it to remain in that original non-existence and it was permissible for that non-existence to change into existence. And when the two matters are equal, the preponderance of one over the other without a preponderator is impossible. So this is what is intended by this proof. And upon understanding it, it becomes apparent that the mentioned objections are invalid.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage concludes a proof from possibility (imkān). The author states that we have necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) that a given entity could have either remained in its original state of non-existence or changed into existence. Since both possibilities are equally possible (tasāwā al-amrān), it is impossible for one to outweigh the other without a preponderator (lā li-murajjiḥ). This is a standard kalam argument for the existence of a creator: the universe could have been or not been, so it requires a cause to tip the balance. The author then says that this is the intended meaning of this proof, and when understood, it becomes clear that the objections raised against it (al-su’ālāt al-madhkūra) are invalid. The passage is a compact summary of the argument and a dismissal of counterarguments.

Key Terms

عدم أصلي
تبدل العدم بالوجود
تساوى الأمران
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The existence of a creator
B) The equal possibility of both states
C) The original non-existence
D) The objections raised

Key Takeaway
Equal possibilities cannot become actual without a preponderator—this is the heart of the proof from possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof says that equal possibilities cannot become actual without a preponderator. What concrete step will you take to ask Allah to tip the balance for you?

Establishing the Cause: Need for a Doer Due to Origination
Author demonstrates that the need for an agent is due to origination (ḥudūth), not pre-eternity or permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for an agent is caused by origination (ḥudūth) and not by pre-eternity or permanence.

Core Idea
The need for a doer is caused by origination, not by prior existence or continuation.

The author now turns to establishing the cause of the need for an agent. He argues that it is not due to pre-eternity or permanence, but solely due to origination. This is a crucial step in proving that the world, being originated, requires a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the proof.
Notice the three possibilities listed.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The proof divides the need into three causes: pre-eternity (qidam), origination (ḥudūth), and permanence (baqā’). Understand why the first and third are rejected.
The author gives two reasons for rejecting permanence.
3
Apply the conclusion to bodies
Since bodies are originated, they must need an agent. See how this argument fits into the larger cosmological proof.
This step links the argument to the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات العلة : فهو أنا ندعي أن احتياج أفعالنا إلينا ، إنما كان لأجل حدوثها . والدليل عليه : أن احتياج أفعالنا إلينا. إما أن يكون لقدمها السابق ، أو لحدوثها ، أو لبقائها . والأول باطل . لأن العدم السابق لا تعلق له بالفاعل . والثالث أيضاً باطل . لوجيهن : الأول : إنه لو احتاج الباقي إلى الفاعل، لكان هذا تحصيلا للحاصل . وهو محال . والثاني : إن البناء قد يبقى بعد فناء الباني، والنقش قد يبقى بعد فناء النقاش . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، علمنا : أن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث . فثبت : أن أفعالنا محتاجة إلينا ، لأجل حدوثها ، وثبت : أن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث. ولما ثبت أن الأجسام محدثة، كانت علة الحاجة إلى الفاعل حاصلة فيها ، فوجب القطع بافتقارها إلى الفاعل . هذا تمام تقرير هذا الدليل .
As for establishing the cause: we claim that the need of our actions for us is only due to their occurrence (ḥudūth). The proof for that: the need of our actions for us is either due to their prior pre-eternity (qidam), or due to their occurrence (ḥudūth), or due to their permanence (baqā’). The first is false because prior non-existence has no relation to the agent. The third is also false for two reasons: first, if the permanent needed the agent, that would be a reacquisition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. Second, a building may remain after the builder's demise, and an engraving may remain after the engraver's demise. Since these two divisions are false, we know that the cause of need is occurrence. So it is established: that our actions need us due to their occurrence, and it is established: that the cause of need is occurrence. And since it is established that bodies are originated (muḥdatha), the cause of the need for an agent is present in them, so it is necessary to be certain of their need for an agent. This completes the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage establishes why actions require an agent. The author proposes a logical division: the need for an agent must be due to one of three: pre-eternity (that the action existed before), origination (coming into existence), or permanence (continuing after existence). He rejects pre-eternity because non-existence has no connection to an agent. He rejects permanence for two reasons: first, if something that already exists needed an agent to continue, that would be a 'reacquisition of what is already obtained', a logical impossibility; second, empirical evidence shows that things like buildings and engravings can remain after their makers are gone. Therefore, the only valid cause is origination (ḥudūth). He then applies this to bodies: since bodies are known to be originated, they must need an agent (Allah). This is a key step in the cosmological argument for God's existence.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة ('illat al-ḥājah)
حدوث (ḥudūth)
قدم (qidam)
بقاء (baqā')
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Permanence also requires a continuous agent for continued existence.
Correction: The author shows that permanence does not require a continuous agent because buildings remain after builders die, and reacquisition of what exists is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that the need for an agent is due to permanence (baqā’)?
A) Because permanence is impossible for originated things.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
C) Because it would require 'reacquisition of what is already obtained' and empirical examples contradict it.
D) Because permanence is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
The cause of the need for an agent is only origination (ḥudūth), so everything that is originated must have a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your entire existence is dependent on a single moment of origination. Do you live each day as if you are a self-sustaining being, or do you truly acknowledge that without the Creator's act of bringing you into existence, you would be nothing? How can you actively remind yourself of this dependency today?
?
The proof shows that permanence does not require an agent. Yet many of us act as if our own good deeds, status, or faith can sustain themselves without Allah's continuous mercy. In what area of your life have you fallen into this illusion, and what concrete step can you take to return to complete reliance on Him?
?
The absurdity of 'reacquiring what is obtained' prevents us from needing an agent for what already exists. Reflect on your relationship with Allah: have you ever tried to 'earn' something He has already given you—like forgiveness, guidance, or love—as if you could acquire it again by your own effort? How does this lesson free you from that burden?

Contingency of Actions and the Need for an Agent
An objection-and-answer arguing that contingency of specific actions does not prove contingency per se is the cause of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a crucial objection to the argument from contingency: the need to distinguish between specific, qualified contingency and contingency per se.

Core Idea
Not all contingency is the same: the cause of need is contingency itself, not qualified contingency.

In this passage, the objector challenges a key step in the argument from contingency. Even if we accept that the contingency of our actions explains their need for us as agents, it does not automatically follow that the contingency of the world explains its need for a creator. The objector forces the defender to clarify whether it is contingency in general or specific contingency that is the cause of need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argumentative structure come through.
Notice the repeated terms حدوث, علة, حاجة.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the objector distinguishes between qualified contingency (حدوث مقيد) and contingency per se. This is the heart of the objection.
The phrase 'حدوث مقيد بقيد كونه حاصلا في أفعالنا' is crucial.
3
Trace the dilemma
Follow the objector's response: if the defender says the premise is intuitive, then the proof is wasteful; if not, then the argument becomes circular.
Look for the words بديهة and دور.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أن حدوث أفعالنا علة لاحتياج أفعالنا إلينا . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم منه أن يكون حدوث العالم علة لاحتياج العالم إلى الفاعل ؟ وتقريره : إن حدوث أفعالنا عبارة عن حدوث مقيد ، بقيد كونه حاصلا في أفعالنا . ولا يلزم من كون هذا المجموع علة للحاجة ، كون الحدوث فقط علة للحاجة . فإن قلتم : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن ذلك الحدوث ما كان علة لتلك الحاجة لكون ذلك الحدوث الخاص ، بل لمجرد كونه حدوثا . فنقول : فهذا إنما يتم لو ثبت لكم : أن الحدوث من حيث هو حدوث ، علة للحاجة . وإن كان ذلك معلوما بالبديهة ، فقد ضاع سعيكم في تقرير هذا الدليل الطويل . وإن كان ذلك محتاجاً إلى الحجة . فأنتم ما ذكرتم إلا هذا القياس . وقد ظهر في هذا السؤال : أن هذا القياس لا يصح ، إلا إذا ثبت أن الحدوث من حيث هو حدوث ، علة للحاجة . وحينئذ يتوقف الدليل على المدلول ، والمدلول على الدليل . فيلزم الدور . وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الموضع . وبالله التوفيق .
The fourth question: We grant that the originatedness of our actions is the cause of the need of our actions for us. Then why do you say that it follows from this that the originatedness of the world is the cause of the world's need for the agent? Its formulation: The originatedness of our actions is originatedness restricted by the restriction of being in our actions. And it does not follow from this composite being the cause of need that originatedness alone is the cause of need. If you say: We know necessarily that that originatedness was not the cause of that need because of that specific originatedness, but merely because it is originatedness. We say: This would only be valid if you establish that originatedness qua originatedness is the cause of need. If that is known by intuition, then your effort in formulating this long argument is wasted. If it is in need of proof, then you have not mentioned except this analogical argument. And it has become evident in this question that this analogical argument is not valid unless it is established that originatedness qua originatedness is the cause of need. Then the evidence depends on the conclusion and the conclusion on the evidence, resulting in circularity. This is the completion of the discussion in this place. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (السؤال الرابع) concerning the causal link between contingency (حدوث) and the need for an agent. The objector grants that the contingency of our actions is the cause of their need for us (the human agent), but questions whether it follows that the contingency of the world is the cause of its need for God. The reason is that the contingency of our actions is qualified (مقيد) by being our actions, so it is a composite. It does not follow that contingency alone (without the qualification) is the cause. The defender of the argument replies that we know intuitively that it is not the specific contingency that causes the need, but contingency as such. The objector then presents a dilemma: if this is intuitive, then the long proof to establish that contingency is the cause is unnecessary; if it is not intuitive, then the proof itself (the qiyas) is insufficient because it relies on that very premise. Thus the argument becomes circular (دور): the proof (that the world needs an agent) depends on the premise that contingency is the cause of need, and that premise is proven by the very argument that uses it. This is a classic kalam critique of causal reasoning.

Key Terms
حدوث
علة
حاجة
مقيد
بديهة
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the contingency of any specific thing automatically proves that contingency in general is the cause of need for an agent.
Correction: The objection shows that the contingency of our actions is qualified (by being ours), and that does not entail that unqualified contingency is the cause. The argument would need an independent proof that contingency per se causes need.

Checkpoint
What does the objector argue would result if the defender claims that originatedness per se is the cause of need by intuition?
A) The intuition is false
B) The proof of the world's need for God is unnecessary
C) The qiyas becomes circular
D) The world would be eternal

Key Takeaway
The objection demonstrates that without an independent proof that contingency per se is the cause of need, the argument from contingency risks circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the reasons for your belief in God's existence, do you rely on an argument that assumes its own conclusion? Examine one of your own beliefs: is the reasoning truly independent, or does it secretly presuppose what you are trying to prove?
?
You have just seen how easily an argument can become circular. Can you identify any area in your own thinking—about God, your purpose, or your values—where you might be guilty of the same error? What would it take to break that cycle and seek real evidence?
?
The objector distinguishes between qualified and unqualified contingency. In your own life, do you ever treat specific, limited experiences as though they prove universal truths? Are you willing to test your assumptions with the same rigorous logic this passage demands?

Proving the Creator from Observed Attributes
Introduction to a proof based on observed attributes in bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses observed attributes in bodies as a starting point to prove the existence of the Creator, even before considering the possibility or origination of the bodies themselves.

Core Idea
Observed changes in bodies point to a Creator, even without prior metaphysical knowledge.

This lesson presents a fresh approach to proving God's existence. Instead of starting with abstract metaphysical concepts like the possibility or origination of bodies, the author begins with something we can all witness: the constant changes and attributes in celestial and elemental bodies. These observable phenomena, which humans cannot produce, serve as direct evidence for a Creator. The passage then classifies these bodies to set the stage for a detailed argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The author states that we can witness attributes that humans cannot produce, and this suffices as evidence for a Creator even before knowing other things.
The author deliberately sets aside the possibility (imkān) and origination (ḥudūth) of bodies as prerequisites.
2
Understand the two categories of bodies
The passage divides all bodies that host these attributes into two: celestial bodies (spheres and planets) and elemental bodies (earth, water, air, fire and their compounds). Note the subdivisions for each.
3
Examine the celestial bodies' attributes
List the observable phenomena associated with celestial bodies: their movements, rotation, rising/setting, day/night, lights/shadows, and changes due to proximity to the zenith and seasons.
These are all regular, complex patterns that cannot be attributed to chance or human agency.
4
Examine the elemental bodies' attributes
For elemental bodies, the author mentions two types: simple (the four elements) and composite. Investigation focuses on their states, combinations, and attributes, and what God has placed in them.
This classification prepares for a teleological argument from design.
5
Reflect on the overall method
Consider how this classification and enumeration of observable attributes serve as a foundation for inferring a wise Creator. The argument is not yet complete but the groundwork is laid.
The author will later conclude from these observations that there must be a Creator who originated and sustains these attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات الحادي والعشرون

إثبات العالم بالصانع بطريقة حدد من الصفات
اعلم : أنا قبل العلم بإمكان [ ذوات ](١) الأجسام وقبل العلم بحدوثها : نشاهد حدوث أحوال وصفات ، لا يقدر البشر عليها . فلا جرم يمكننا أن نستدل بها على وجود الصانع
فاعلم : أن الأجسام التي هي المحال لهذه الحوادث المشاهدة المحسوسة : إما أن تكون هي الأجسام الفلكية ، أو الأجسام العنصـريـة . أما الأجسام الأفلاك والكواكب. والبحث عنها إما أن يقع في كيفية حركاتها الفلكية فهي ودورانها وطلوع الكواكب وغروبها (٢) . وإما أن تقع بحسب الليل والنهار ، واعتبار أحوال الأضواء والإظلال (٣) وأما تقع بحسب الأحوال المختلفة التي تعرض للكواكب بسبب قربها أو بعدها من سمت الرؤوس ، وبسبب المصالح الحاصلة من الفصول الأربعة .

The twenty-first discourse
On
Proving the world has a Creator by way of determining from attributes
Know: before knowing the possibility of the essences of bodies and before knowing their origination, we witness the occurrence of states and attributes that humans are not able to produce. Hence we can infer from them the existence of the Creator.
Know: The bodies which are the loci for these witnessed sensible events are either the celestial bodies or the elemental bodies. As for the bodies of the spheres and planets, investigation concerning them either falls into the manner of their celestial movements, their rotation, the rising and setting of the planets; or according to night and day and consideration of the states of lights and shadows; or according to the various states that occur to the planets due to their proximity or distance from the zenith, and due to the benefits arising from the four seasons.
As for the elemental bodies, they are either simple or composite. As for the simple ones, investigation is about the states of the four elements, their combinations, their attributes, and the manner of what God has deposited in them.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage begins a new proof for the existence of God based on observed attributes (ṣifāt) in bodies. The author states that even before we know the possibility (imkān) or origination (ḥudūth) of bodies, we can directly observe the occurrence of states and attributes (aḥwāl wa ṣifāt) that humans cannot produce. These observable changes serve as evidence for a Creator. The bodies that host these events are of two kinds: celestial (falakiyyah) and elemental (ʿunṣuriyyah). For celestial bodies (spheres and planets), investigation covers their movements, rotation, rising and setting, day and night, lights and shadows, and their varying positions relative to the zenith and the four seasons. For elemental bodies, they are either simple (basāʾiṭ) like the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) or composite (murakkabāt). The simple ones are studied by examining their states, combinations, and attributes, as well as what God has placed in them. The passage thus sets up a classification of observable phenomena that will be used to argue for a wise Creator, though the actual argument is not yet complete in this excerpt.

الأجسام العنصرية

Correction: The author deliberately starts before that knowledge, relying solely on observed attributes.

Key Takeaway
Observed attributes in celestial and elemental bodies, which humans cannot produce, provide a direct and accessible proof for the existence of a Creator.
